    
ECLIPSE OF FIRE & WINGS
Book One of The Phoenix & Peacock Chronicles
Hannah Kraulik Pagade

PRELUDE

In a world where spirit and flesh are one,
where souls manifest as beasts of ancient power,
seven pairs were destined to rise.


Bonded by fate. Bound by love.
Separated by shadow. United by fire.


For three thousand years, the world forgot.
Forgot the prophecy.
Forgot the price that was paid.
Forgot that darkness, once imprisoned, always hungers to be free.


But destiny remembers.


And when the first pair awakensâ€”
when Phoenix flame meets Peacock's grace,
when fire finds its healing lightâ€”


The hunt begins.

Some bonds are written in starlight.
Some loves transcend death itself.
And some destinies burn so bright,
the whole world must rise or fall in their wake.

CHAPTER ONE: THE DREAM OF FIRE AND FEATHERS
The sky bled crimson and gold, colors too violent to be beautiful, too vivid to be real. Vihan stood at the center of a world that had long since stopped making senseâ€”a battlefield of memory and nightmare woven together so tightly he could no longer tell where one ended and the other began.
Smoke coiled around his boots, thick and choking, carrying the acrid stench of burning wood and something far worse. Ash. Bone. The remnants of things that should have been left buried.
He had been here before. Every night for the past three years, this place called to him, dragged him down into its depths whether he willed it or not. The dreamscape never changed. The flames always burned in the same pattern. The ruins stretched endlessly in every direction, jagged silhouettes of what had once been towers, temples, homes.
And she was always there.
Waiting.
Vihan's jaw tightened, his hands curling into fists at his sides even though he knewâ€”knewâ€”that this was not real. That his body lay sleeping in his tent miles away, surrounded by his warriors, protected by steel and strategy.
But knowledge meant nothing here.
Here, in this place between waking and oblivion, she ruled.
The laughter came first. Soft, lilting, sweet as poisoned honey. It drifted through the smoke like a caress, wrapping around his throat, his chest, squeezing until his breath came shallow and quick.
"Did you miss me, my love?"
The voice slithered through the air, neither fully human nor fully beast, but something twisted in between. It dripped with false affection, with the memory of a tenderness that had never truly existed, only mimicked until he'd been foolish enough to believe it real.
Vihan did not turn. He would not give her the satisfaction.
"You're not real," he said, his voice steady despite the thunder of his pulse. "This is a dream. Nothing more."
"Is it?"
The flames parted like a curtain drawn aside, and she emerged from the inferno as though born from it.
Sharvani.
But not as she had been in lifeâ€”not the woman with dark eyes and a sharper tongue, not the wife who had once stood beside him in victory and whispered promises of empire.
No.
Here, she was the lioness.
Golden fur gleamed like molten metal in the firelight, each strand catching the glow of the flames until she seemed to burn from within. Her body moved with a predator's grace, muscles rippling beneath her pelt, shoulders rolling with each deliberate step. Her eyesâ€”those eyes that had once looked at him with something he'd mistaken for loveâ€”were hollow now. Empty. Reflecting nothing but her own insatiable hunger.
She circled him slowly, her gaze never leaving his face.
"You cannot run from me, Vihan." Her voice was a purr, deceptively gentle. "You never could. Do you know why?"
He said nothing.
She continued as though he had answered.
"Because you are nothing without me." The words were spoken with such certainty, such conviction, that for a momentâ€”just a momentâ€”he almost believed them. "You were a soldier. A warrior with a sword and no purpose. I gave you purpose. I made you the Phoenix."
"You broke me," Vihan said quietly, his voice like gravel underfoot. "There's a difference."
The lioness laughed, the sound sharp and cruel. "Broke you? No, my love. I forged you. Every battle you won, every enemy you felled, every crown you laid at my feetâ€”that was my work. You were clay, and I shaped you into something magnificent."
The heat around him intensified, the flames pressing closer, licking at the edges of his awareness. He could feel the weight of the past bearing down on him, the memories she dredged up with every word. The nights he had knelt before her, seeking approval that never came. The wars he had fought not for honor, but for her ambition. The slow, suffocating realization that the woman he loved had never seen him as a partner, only a weapon to be wielded.
"You were mine," Sharvani whispered, leaning closer, her breath hot against his neck. "And you will be again. This... peace you think you've found? This quiet life you're pretending to live?" She laughed, the sound echoing through the ruins. "It's a lie. You are fire, Vihan. And fire does not rest. It consumes."
Vihan's breath hitched, his chest tightening. The flames roared higher, the heat unbearable now, and he could feel the dream pulling him deeper, threatening to drown him in the suffocating weight of her presence.
"I don't need you," he said, but the words sounded hollow even to his own ears.
"Don't you?" The lioness tilted her head, her gaze sharp and knowing. "Then why do I still haunt your dreams?"
The question struck deeper than any blade.
Because she was right.
Three years since he had walked away. Three years since he had severed every tie, burned every bridge, left her standing in the ruins of the empire they had built together.
And still, she followed him.
Not in flesh. Not in waking.
But here.
Always here.
The flames surged, rising higher, higher, until the world was nothing but heat and smoke and the suffocating weight of her voiceâ€”
And then, something shifted.
The fire wavered.
Vihan's eyes snapped openâ€”or perhaps they had always been open; it was impossible to tell in this placeâ€”and he felt it.
A change in the air. A ripple, like a stone dropped into still water.
The flames bent. Not away from him, but toward something else. Something new.
The lioness snarled, her ears flattening against her skull, her body tensing. "No."
A light bloomed in the distance, cutting through the smoke like a blade through silk.
Sapphire blue. Deep and endless, the color of the sky before a storm, of water in a hidden spring untouched by human hands. It pulsed once, twice, each beat steady and calm, a heartbeat in opposition to the chaos around him.
Vihan's breath stuttered.
The light grew brighter, and from within it, a figure began to take shape.
A woman.
She moved through the inferno as though it could not touch her, each step deliberate and unhurried. The flames recoiled from her presence, bending and twisting as if in deference. Her form shimmered, not quite solid, not quite etherealâ€”somewhere in between, as though she existed in a place beyond the boundaries of this nightmare.
She was draped in silk that flowed like water, shifting between gold and the deepest blue with every movement. The fabric clung to her form, revealing the gentle curve of her shoulders, the graceful line of her neck. And at her backâ€”
Feathers.
Iridescent. Shimmering. A cascade of brilliant plumage that fanned outward in a display of impossible beauty. They caught the firelight and reflected it back a hundredfold, each feather glowing with an inner radiance that seemed to hold the very essence of dawn.
A peacock.
The woman stopped a few paces away, and for the first time in years, Vihan felt the choking grip around his chest loosen.
She was... calm. Not in the way of someone untouched by suffering, but in the way of someone who had learned to carry it without being consumed.
Her eyes met his.
Dark. Deep. Knowing.
And gods, the way she looked at himâ€”
Not with pity. Not with judgment.
But with recognition.
As though she saw past the armor, past the scars, past the man he pretended to be, and looked directly at the soul beneath.
"You are not who she says you are," the woman said softly, her voice like the first gentle rain after a drought.
Vihan's throat tightened. He wanted to speak, to ask who she was, what she was, but the words tangled in his chest, trapped beneath years of silence and shame.
The lioness roared, the sound shaking the very foundations of the dreamscape. "You do not belong here!" Sharvani's voice was no longer sweet, no longer seductive. It was raw fury, stripped of pretense. "He is mine!"
The woman did not flinch. She did not even turn to acknowledge the lioness.
Instead, she took another step forward, her gaze never leaving Vihan's.
"You have been carrying her voice for too long," she murmured. "But it is not the only voice you must hear."
Sharvani lunged.
The lioness moved with terrifying speed, claws extended, fangs bared, every inch of her a weapon honed by rage and obsession.
But the woman raised a single hand.
Light erupted from her palm, golden and blue, intertwining like twin flames. The air crackled with powerâ€”not violent, not destructive, but absolute. The kind of power that did not need to shout to be heard.
The lioness collided with the barrier of light and recoiled, snarling, her form flickering like a candle in the wind.
"No," Sharvani hissed, her voice breaking, desperate now. "No, you cannotâ€”he is mineâ€”"
But her voice was fading, growing distant, as though the dream itself was rejecting her presence.
The flames began to die.
The smoke thinned.
And for the first time in three years, Vihan felt the oppressive weight lift from his chest.
The woman lowered her hand, the light dimming but not disappearing entirely. She turned back to Vihan, and this time, there was something softer in her gaze. Something almost... tender.
"You are stronger than you know," she said quietly. "But you have forgotten how to see it."
Vihan's voice finally found him. "Who are you?"
The corner of her lips curvedâ€”not quite a smile, but something close. Something that held both sorrow and hope in equal measure.
"I think," she said softly, "you already know."
She reached up, her fingers hovering just over his chestâ€”not touching, but close enough that he could feel the warmth radiating from her hand. The light pulsed once more, and he felt something deep within him stir. A part of himself he had thought long dead.
"Find me," she whispered.
The dream shattered.


Vihan jolted awake with a gasp, his body drenched in sweat, his heart hammering against his ribs like a war drum. His hand shot to his chest, fingers splaying over the place where her hand had almost touched, expectingâ€”hopingâ€”to find something. A mark. A burn. Anything to prove it had been real.
There was nothing.
But gods, he could still feel her.
Not Sharvani's suffocating presence, but hers. The woman in blue and gold. The peacock.
He sat up slowly, dragging a hand through his damp hair, his breath still uneven. The tent around him was dark, the camp outside quiet save for the distant crackle of dying fires and the low murmur of guards on watch.
It was just a dream.
And yet.
He could still smell sandalwood and jasmine, faint and fleeting, as though she had truly been there.
"Find me."
The words echoed in his mind, not as a command, but as a promise. An invitation.
Vihan exhaled slowly, his hands trembling as he pressed them against his knees, grounding himself in the here and now. He had spent three years running from the past. Three years trying to outrun Sharvani's ghost.
But thisâ€”
This was different.
For the first time since he had walked away from everything he had known, he did not feel haunted.
He felt... called.
Across the tent, a low voice broke the silence.
"You're awake."
Vihan's gaze snapped to the entrance, where Varun stood silhouetted against the faint glow of the campfires outside. His oldest friend, his brother in all but blood, regarded him with a mixture of concern and curiosity.
"You were talking in your sleep," Varun said, stepping inside. "Something about finding someone."
Vihan did not answer immediately. He couldn't. Because how could he explain what he had just experienced? How could he put into words the feeling of drowning for years, only to finallyâ€”finallyâ€”break the surface and breathe?
"I need to leave," Vihan said at last, his voice rough.
Varun's brows rose. "Leave? We just secured the northern border. The menâ€”"
"The men will be fine," Vihan interrupted, rising to his feet. "You'll lead them. You always have."
Varun studied him for a long moment, his sharp gaze searching Vihan's face for answers. "Where are you going?"
Vihan turned toward the tent's opening, where the first pale light of dawn was beginning to creep over the horizon.
"I don't know," he admitted. "But I'll know when I find her."
Varun said nothing for a moment. Then, quietly: "Her?"
Vihan's jaw tightened, his hands curling into fists at his sides.
"Yes," he said simply.
And for the first time in three years, Vihan did not feel like a man running from something.
He felt like a man finally running toward it.



CHAPTER TWO: THE HEALER'S BURDEN
ðŸ”¥   âœ¨
  
The first light of dawn crept over the temple walls like a whispered secret, soft and golden, painting the ancient stone in shades of amber and rose. The air was cool and clean, carrying with it the scent of night-blooming jasmine and the earthy promise of dew-dampened soil.
Aaravi had been awake for hours.
She sat in the courtyard garden, her legs folded beneath her, her hands moving with practiced efficiency as she ground dried herbs in a worn mortar. The rhythmic scrape of pestle against stone was a meditation in itself, a sound so familiar it had become part of her heartbeat. Valerian root for calming tinctures. Turmeric for inflammation. Neem leaves for wounds that refused to heal.
Around her, the temple still slept. The monks would not rise for another hour, and the wounded would not wake until the sun climbed higher, chasing away the last remnants of night. This timeâ€”this quiet, sacred space between darkness and dayâ€”belonged to her alone.
Or it should have.
But today, the peace she usually found in these moments eluded her.
Her hands moved mechanically, muscle memory guiding her fingers while her mind drifted elsewhere. Somewhere darker. Somewhere she had spent years trying not to go.
She could still see his face.
Not clearly. Not the way one remembered a loved one or a friend. But in fragments, in flashesâ€”gold eyes reflecting firelight, a jaw tight with pain, shoulders that carried a weight no mortal should bear.
The man from her dream.
Aaravi's hands stilled.
It had been three nights now since the first dream. Three nights of falling asleep in her own bed only to wake in a world of fire and fury, standing in the center of an inferno that should have burned her alive but somehow... didn't.
And he was always there.
Trapped. Drowning. Fighting against chains she couldn't see but could feelâ€”gods, she could feel them, wrapped around his chest, his throat, his very soul.
She had tried to dismiss it at first. Dreams were dreams. The mind played tricks, weaving stories from exhaustion and stress. She had been working too hard, sleeping too little. That was all.
But then the second night came.
And the third.
And each time, the dream grew stronger. More vivid. More real.
Last night, she had reached for him. Had stepped through the flames as though they were nothing more than mist, her hand outstretched, her voice calling out even though she didn't know his name.
And when she touched himâ€”when her fingers had hovered just over his heartâ€”she had felt it.
The bond.
Ancient. Undeniable. Woven into the fabric of something far older than memory.
Aaravi exhaled slowly, setting the pestle down with trembling hands.
"You're distracted."
The voice came from behind her, sharp and knowing, cutting through her thoughts like a blade through silk.
Aaravi didn't turn. She didn't need to.
"Good morning, Mira," she said softly, reaching for a bundle of dried chamomile.
Mira stepped into view, her silver hair pulled back in a neat braid, her robes the color of storm clouds. She was older than the temple itself, or so the stories claimed, though Aaravi suspected Mira encouraged such tales simply because she enjoyed being mysterious. Her face was lined with age, but her eyesâ€”sharp and dark and impossibly perceptiveâ€”missed nothing.
She settled onto the stone bench across from Aaravi with a grace that belied her years, folding her hands in her lap.
"You've been grinding that same batch of valerian for the past ten minutes," Mira observed mildly. "Either you're making enough sedative to knock out an elephant, or your mind is elsewhere."
Aaravi glanced down at the mortar. Mira was right. The herbs had been reduced to powder so fine it was nearly dust.
She set the pestle aside with a sigh.
"I didn't sleep well," she admitted.
"Clearly." Mira tilted her head, her gaze sharpening. "The same dream?"
Aaravi hesitated, then nodded.
She had told Mira about the first dream two days ago, half expecting her mentor to dismiss it as exhaustion or an overactive imagination. But Mira had gone very still, her expression unreadable, before asking a single question:
Did you see his face?
Aaravi had said yes.
Mira had said nothing more.
Now, the older woman studied her with an intensity that made Aaravi want to squirm.
"Tell me," Mira said quietly. "What happened this time?"
Aaravi's fingers twisted in her lap, a nervous habit she'd never quite outgrown.
"There was a woman," she said slowly, choosing her words carefully. "A lioness. Golden. Beautiful. But... wrong. Twisted." She paused, remembering the way the creature had circled the man, the possessive hunger in her hollow eyes. "She was hurting him. Not with claws or fangs, but with words. With memories."
Mira's expression darkened, but she said nothing.
"I stepped between them," Aaravi continued, her voice barely above a whisper. "I don't know why. I just... I couldn't stand there and watch. And when I did, the lioness tried to attack me, butâ€”" She broke off, shaking her head. "Light came from my hands. Blue and gold. It drove her back."
"And the man?" Mira asked.
Aaravi's throat tightened.
"He looked at me like..." She trailed off, struggling to find the words. "Like he'd been drowning, and I was air."
The silence that followed was heavy, weighted with something Aaravi couldn't name.
Finally, Mira spoke, her voice low and grave.
"This is not a simple dream, child."
Aaravi had known that. Deep down, she had known from the first night. But hearing Mira say it aloud made it real in a way it hadn't been before.
"Then what is it?" she asked.
Mira rose from the bench, moving to the edge of the garden where the morning light spilled over the stone walls. She was quiet for a long moment, her gaze distant, as though looking at something far beyond the temple grounds.
"There are bonds," Mira said at last, "that exist outside the realm of choice. They are written into the fabric of the world itself, woven by forces older than gods or men. Most people live their entire lives without ever encountering such a thing. But for those who do..." She turned back to Aaravi, her expression solemn. "It changes everything."
Aaravi's heart hammered in her chest.
"You're saying this is real," she whispered. "He's real."
"Yes."
The single word landed like a stone in still water, sending ripples through everything Aaravi thought she knew.
"Where is he?" she asked, her voice barely audible.
Mira's lips curved into a faint, knowing smile.
"If the dreams are trueâ€”and I believe they areâ€”then he is looking for you. And when he finds you, you will have a choice to make."
"What choice?"
Mira's gaze softened, something almost like pity flickering in her eyes.
"Whether to answer the call," she said simply. "Or turn away from it."
Before Aaravi could respond, footsteps echoed through the courtyard. She turned to see her father, Ravindra, approaching from the temple's inner sanctum. He was a tall man, his dark hair streaked with silver, his face kind despite the weariness etched into the lines around his eyes.
He had been the temple's head healer for nearly thirty years, and in that time, he had saved countless lives. But the work had taken its toll. Aaravi could see it in the way his shoulders slumped, in the careful slowness of his movements.
She rose immediately, brushing the herb dust from her hands.
"Father," she greeted, concern threading through her voice. "You should be resting. The council meeting isn't until midday."
Ravindra waved a dismissive hand, though his smile was warm.
"I've rested enough for three lifetimes," he said, settling onto the bench Mira had vacated. "Besides, I wanted to speak with you."
Aaravi exchanged a glance with Mira, who merely raised an eyebrow and excused herself with a knowing look.
Once they were alone, Ravindra patted the space beside him.
Aaravi sat, her hands folding in her lap, waiting.
Her father was quiet for a moment, his gaze drifting over the gardenâ€”the carefully tended herbs, the flowering vines climbing the temple walls, the small fountain trickling softly in the corner.
"You've been working too hard," he said at last.
Aaravi opened her mouth to protest, but he held up a hand.
"I know you, daughter. You give and give until there is nothing left. You heal everyone who comes through these gates, but you never stop to ask if you, yourself, need healing."
The words struck deeper than she expected.
"I'm fine," she said quietly, though even she could hear the lie in her voice.
Ravindra turned to her, his dark eyes searching her face.
"Are you?"
Aaravi looked away, her throat tightening.
No.
She wasn't fine.
She hadn't been fine in a long time.
But how could she explain that to him? How could she tell her fatherâ€”who had spent his entire life healing others, who had taught her everything she knew about medicine and compassionâ€”that sometimes, she felt like an empty vessel? That she gave so much of herself to others that she no longer knew what was left?
"I had a brother," she said suddenly, the words spilling out before she could stop them. "I don't remember him. I was too young. But I know he existed. I know he died."
Ravindra's expression softened, pain flickering across his features.
"You were only two years old when he passed," he said gently. "You couldn't have known him."
"But Mother did."
The name hung between them, heavy and fragile.
Rani.
Aaravi's mother.
A woman who had once been vibrant and full of life, or so the stories claimed. But the woman Aaravi knew was a ghost, a hollow shell who moved through the world but never truly lived in it.
She had lost a son, and in losing him, she had lost herself.
And in the years that followed, she had never quite found her way back.
"She's never looked at me the same way," Aaravi whispered. "I've spent my whole life trying to be enough. To fill the space he left. But I never could."
Ravindra's hand covered hers, warm and steady.
"You were never meant to fill that space, Aaravi," he said quietly. "You were meant to be yourself. But your mother..." He trailed off, his voice thick with old grief. "She doesn't know how to let go of what she's lost. And in holding on so tightly, she's missed what's right in front of her."
Aaravi blinked back the sting of tears.
"I don't know how to stop giving," she admitted. "Even when it hurts. Even when I'm empty. I don't know how to just... be."
Ravindra squeezed her hand gently.
"Then perhaps," he said, "it's time you learned."


The rest of the morning passed in a blur.
Aaravi moved through her duties with mechanical precisionâ€”changing bandages, brewing tinctures, tending to the small wounds and ailments of the temple residents. Her hands were steady, her voice calm, her presence a balm to those who sought her care.
But inside, her thoughts churned.
Find me.
The words echoed in her mind, over and over, until they became a drumbeat she couldn't silence.
She tried to push them away. Tried to focus on the work in front of her.
But the pull was too strong.
By the time the sun reached its zenith, Aaravi found herself standing at the edge of the temple's highest terrace, her hands gripping the stone railing, her gaze fixed on the distant horizon.
Somewhere out there, he was looking for her.
She could feel it.
Not in any logical, rational way. But in the same way she could feel a storm approaching before the first clouds appeared. In the same way she could sense a fever breaking before the patient woke.
It was instinct.
It was fate.
And for the first time in her life, Aaravi allowed herself to acknowledge the truth she had been running from.
She was tired of being invisible.
Tired of giving pieces of herself away until there was nothing left.
Tired of being the healer who never allowed herself to be healed.
If he cameâ€”if this man from her dreams truly existedâ€”then she would answer.
Not because fate demanded it.
But because she chose it.


That night, Aaravi lay in her bed, staring up at the ceiling, her heart pounding in her chest.
She didn't know if she would dream again.
Didn't know if he would be there, waiting in that world of fire and shadow.
But as her eyes drifted closed, as sleep pulled her down into its depths, she whispered the words aloudâ€”a promise, a prayer, a declaration.
"I'm here. Find me."
And in the space between waking and dreaming, she felt it.
A pulse of warmth. A flicker of light.
The bond, reaching back.
He was coming.



CHAPTER THREE: THE GHOST OF HER
ðŸ”¥   âœ¨
  
The war tent smelled of leather, smoke, and old maps.
Vihan stood at the head of the long wooden table, his hands braced against its scarred surface, his eyes fixed on the carefully drawn lines that marked their territory. Red flags pinned strategic positions. Blue marked water sources. Black indicated enemy movements from the last three months.
All of it felt impossibly distant.
Around him, his officers spoke in low, clipped tonesâ€”discussing supply routes, guard rotations, the fortification of the northern pass. These were men he had fought beside for years, men whose lives he had saved and who had saved his in turn. Their voices blended together into a steady hum of military efficiency, the kind of conversation he could usually navigate in his sleep.
But today, every word felt like it was coming from underwater.
"â€”recommend doubling the night watch on the eastern ridgeâ€”"
"â€”supplies from the southern outpost should arrive by week's endâ€”"
"â€”reports of bandits near the trade routes, nothing we can't handleâ€”"
Vihan heard the words, but they didn't land.
His mind was elsewhere.
In a dream that felt more real than the waking world.
With a woman he had never met but somehow knew.
"Vihan."
The voice cut through the fog, sharp and familiar.
He looked up to find Varun watching him from across the table, his dark eyes narrowed in that particular way that meant he'd noticed something amiss. The others had fallen silent, all eyes now turned toward him, waiting.
Vihan straightened, forcing his attention back to the present.
"Repeat that," he said, his voice steady despite the chaos in his mind.
Ishaan, his third-in-command, exchanged a glance with Varun before clearing his throat.
"I was saying, sir, that the scouts reported movement along the western border. It doesn't appear hostile, butâ€”"
"Send a patrol," Vihan interrupted. "Twenty men. Report back within two days."
Ishaan nodded, making a note.
"And the supply situation?" another officer asked.
"Manageable," Vihan said curtly. "Ration carefully. We're not expecting conflict, but we prepare as if we are."
The meeting continued for another hour, each decision made with the same mechanical precision Vihan had relied on for years. Strategy. Logistics. Discipline.
It was what he was good at.
What he had been good at.
But as the officers filed out of the tent one by one, their tasks assigned and their orders clear, Vihan felt the weight of something shifting inside him. A tectonic movement beneath the surface, slow but inevitable.
Varun lingered.
He always did.
Once the tent was empty save for the two of them, Varun closed the flap and turned, his arms crossed over his chest, his expression unreadable.
"Alright," he said. "What's going on?"
Vihan didn't look at him. He moved to the small table in the corner where a pitcher of water sat alongside a half-empty bottle of whiskey. He poured himself a cup of water, drained it, and poured another.
"Nothing," he said.
Varun huffed a quiet laugh, though there was no humor in it.
"You've been distracted for three days. You barely speak unless spoken to. You stare at maps like they're written in a language you no longer understand." He paused. "And this morning, I heard you talking in your sleep."
Vihan's hand stilled on the pitcher.
"What did I say?"
"You kept repeating the same thing. Over and over." Varun's voice softened. "Find me."
The words hung in the air between them, fragile and impossibly heavy.
Vihan set the pitcher down carefully, his jaw tightening.
"It was just a dream."
"Was it?"
Vihan turned to face his oldest friend, the man who had stood beside him through every battle, every loss, every moment of doubt. Varun's expression was patient but unyielding, the look of someone who knew a lie when he heard it.
"I don't know," Vihan admitted finally, his voice rough. "I don't know what it was."
Varun studied him for a long moment before gesturing to the chairs near the brazier.
"Sit. Talk."
Vihan hesitated, then complied. He sank into the chair with a weariness that had nothing to do with physical exhaustion, his elbows resting on his knees, his head bowed.
Varun sat across from him, waiting.
The silence stretched, broken only by the faint crackle of the dying coals in the brazier.
Finally, Vihan spoke.
"Three nights ago, I had a dream." He paused, the words catching in his throat. "It started the same way it always does. Fire. Smoke. Ruins. Her."
He didn't need to say her name.
Varun knew.
Everyone who had served under Vihan knew.
Sharvani. The lioness. The woman who had shaped him and shattered him in equal measure.
"But this time," Vihan continued, his voice barely above a whisper, "something was different."
"Different how?"
Vihan lifted his head, his golden eyes haunted.
"There was someone else. A woman. She stepped into the fire, andâ€”" He broke off, shaking his head as if trying to dislodge the memory. "She wasn't afraid. Not of the flames. Not of Sharvani. She just... stood there. And when Sharvani attacked her, she raised her hand, and light came from her palm. Blue and gold."
Varun's brows furrowed.
"Light?"
"Not fire. Not lightning. Just... light." Vihan's hands clenched into fists. "It drove Sharvani back. The lioness couldn't touch her. And when she looked at meâ€”" His voice cracked. "When she looked at me, it was like she saw me. Not the soldier. Not the weapon. Just... me."
The silence that followed was profound.
Varun leaned back in his chair, his expression thoughtful.
"And you think this woman is real," he said slowly.
"I know she is."
"How?"
Vihan met his gaze, unflinching.
"Because I can still feel her."
Varun exhaled, dragging a hand through his hair.
"Vihan, I've known you for fifteen years. I've seen you face down armies without flinching. I've watched you walk through fireâ€”literal fireâ€”and come out the other side. But this..." He gestured vaguely. "This is different. You're talking about a dream. A vision. How do you know it's not justâ€”"
"It's not Sharvani playing tricks," Vihan interrupted, his voice hard. "I know what that feels like. This is... the opposite of that."
Varun was quiet for a long moment.
Then, carefully: "What are you going to do?"
Vihan didn't hesitate.
"I'm going to find her."
Varun's eyes widened.
"You don't even know where she is."
"I'll know."
"How?"
Vihan rose from his chair, moving to the tent's entrance. He pulled the flap aside, staring out at the camp beyondâ€”rows of tents, cook fires burning low, soldiers moving through their routines with the ease of long practice.
This had been his life for as long as he could remember.
War. Strategy. Survival.
But standing here now, staring at the familiar landscape of his existence, he felt nothing.
No purpose. No pull.
Only the echo of two words, whispered in a voice that wasn't his own.
Find me.
"I have to leave," Vihan said quietly.
Behind him, Varun was silent.
Then: "When?"
"Tomorrow morning."
"Vihanâ€”"
"I need to do this, Varun." Vihan turned to face him, and for the first time in years, there was something in his expression that wasn't carefully controlled. Something raw. Desperate. "I've been running from the past for three years. Running from her ghost, from the things she made me believe about myself. And all that time, I thought I was moving forward. But I wasn't. I was just... standing still. Drowning."
He took a breath.
"This womanâ€”whoever she isâ€”she pulled me out of that water. And I need to know why. I need to know if she's real. Because if she is..." He trailed off, his voice breaking. "If she is, then maybe I'm not as broken as I thought."
The tent was quiet save for the distant sounds of the camp.
Finally, Varun rose, crossing the space between them. He placed a hand on Vihan's shoulder, his grip firm and steady.
"Then go," he said simply. "Find her. And when you doâ€”" He paused, his expression softening. "When you do, hold on to her. Don't let her go."
Vihan's throat tightened.
"What if she doesn't exist?"
Varun's lips curved into a faint smile.
"She exists. I can see it in your eyes. You've already found her. You just haven't reached her yet."


The rest of the day passed in a blur of preparation.
Vihan packed lightâ€”travel rations, a bedroll, his sword, a small pouch of coin. He left orders with Varun to maintain the camp in his absence, to hold the line until he returned.
If he returned.
The thought should have unsettled him, but it didn't.
For the first time in years, he felt something other than the hollow ache of duty.
He felt purpose.
As the sun dipped below the horizon, painting the sky in shades of crimson and gold, Vihan stood at the edge of the camp, staring out at the road that stretched into the distance.
And that was when she appeared.
Not in the flesh.
But in his mind.
A flicker at the edge of his vision, golden fur catching the dying light.
The lioness.
Sharvani.
She materialized slowly, her form shimmering like heat waves over sand, until she stood before him in all her terrible beauty. The woman he had loved. The monster he had fled.
Her hollow eyes fixed on him, and when she spoke, her voice was silk over steel.
"Leaving, my love?"
Vihan didn't flinch.
"You're not real," he said quietly.
"Aren't I?" She circled him slowly, her movements languid, predatory. "I'm in every scar on your body. Every decision you've made. Every breath you take." She leaned closer, her voice dropping to a whisper. "You can't escape me, Vihan. I'm part of you."
"You were part of me," Vihan corrected, his voice steady. "But not anymore."
The lioness laughed, a sound like breaking glass.
"Is that what you tell yourself? That you're free?" She shook her head. "You're chasing a ghost, Vihan. A dream. A fantasy of salvation that doesn't exist. And when you don't find herâ€”when you realize she was nothing but smoke and shadowâ€”you'll come back to me."
Vihan turned to face her fully, his golden eyes burning.
"I'm not coming back."
Sharvani's expression flickeredâ€”just for a momentâ€”with something that might have been pain. Or rage. It was impossible to tell.
"You think this woman in your dreams will save you?" she hissed. "You think she'll see the man you truly are and not run? You're a weapon, Vihan. That's all you've ever been. That's all you'll ever be. And weapons don't get to be loved."
The words struck deep, finding the cracks in his armor with practiced ease.
But this time, they didn't take root.
"Maybe you're right," Vihan said quietly. "Maybe I am a weapon. Maybe that's all I know how to be." He paused, his voice hardening. "But she looked at me, and she didn't see that. She saw something else. And I'm going to find out what."
The lioness snarled, her form flickering.
"She'll break you worse than I ever did."
"Then I'll break."
Sharvani stared at him for a long moment, her hollow eyes searching his face for somethingâ€”fear, doubt, hesitation.
She found none.
And with a final, furious shriek, she dissolved into nothing, leaving only the scent of smoke and ash in her wake.
Vihan exhaled slowly, his hands unclenching.
She would return. She always did.
But each time, her hold on him weakened.
And one day, he would be free.
Behind him, footsteps approached.
Varun.
"Talking to ghosts again?" his friend asked, his tone light but his eyes concerned.
Vihan managed a faint smile.
"Old habits."
Varun clapped him on the shoulder.
"The horse is ready. Supplies are packed. You can leave at first light."
Vihan nodded, his gaze drifting back to the horizon.
Somewhere out there, beyond the mountains and forests and rivers he would have to cross, she was waiting.
The woman in blue and gold.
The peacock.
The one who had reached into his nightmare and pulled him toward the light.
"Find me," she had whispered.
And he would.
Even if it took him to the ends of the earth.
Even if it destroyed him.
He would find her.



CHAPTER FOUR: THE PATH CALLS
ðŸ”¥   âœ¨
  
The herb garden was Aaravi's sanctuary, but today it felt like a cage.
She knelt among the rows of medicinal plants, her hands working automaticallyâ€”pruning dead leaves, checking for pests, watering the thirstier specimensâ€”but her mind was leagues away. The morning sun warmed her back, and the air was thick with the mingled scents of lavender, rosemary, and holy basil, yet none of it brought the usual peace.
Her hands trembled as she reached for a bunch of tulsi leaves.
Three drops of the tincture she'd been preparing splashed onto the stone pathway instead of into the vial.
She cursed softly under her breath.
This was the fourth mistake she'd made this morning.
Fourth.
Aaravi pressed her palms against her thighs, forcing herself to breathe slowly. In through the nose. Out through the mouth. The way her father had taught her when she was a child prone to anxious spirals.
But it didn't help.
Because the dream hadn't faded with the dawn.
If anything, it had grown stronger.
She could still feel the heat of those flames on her skin, still smell the smoke and ash. Still see his faceâ€”those golden eyes that burned with something between desperation and hope. Still hear his voice, rough and broken, asking a question he hadn't spoken aloud.
Are you real?
And gods help her, she had wanted to answer.
Yes. I'm real. I'm here. Find me.
"You're going to poison someone if you keep working like this."
Aaravi's head snapped up.
Mira stood at the edge of the garden, her arms crossed, her expression somewhere between amused and concerned. She wore her traditional grey robes, her silver hair braided and pinned in an elaborate crown that spoke of formality. Which meant she'd just come from morning prayers with the temple elders.
Which meant she'd been watching Aaravi fumble through her work for who knew how long.
"I'm fine," Aaravi said automatically, setting down the vial before she could spill anything else.
Mira raised one elegant eyebrow.
"You just added valerian root to what should be a fever reducer. Unless you're trying to put the patient to sleep and lower their temperature simultaneously, I'd say you're very much not fine."
Aaravi looked down at the mortar and pestle in front of her.
Mira was right.
She'd been so distracted she'd grabbed the wrong herb entirely.
"Damn it," she muttered, pushing the mixture aside.

"How many nights now?"
Aaravi didn't pretend to misunderstand.

"And the man?"
Aaravi's hands stilled.
"He's looking for me. I can feel it. Not just in the dreams, but..." She pressed a hand to her chest, just over her heart. "Here. Like there's a thread connecting us, pulling tighter with every passing hour."
Mira was quiet for a moment, her dark eyes thoughtful.
Then she asked the question Aaravi had been dreading.
"Have you felt your spirit animal stir?"
The words landed like a stone in still water.
Aaravi's breath stopped.
She had been hopingâ€”prayingâ€”that Mira wouldn't ask. That she could somehow avoid this conversation for just a little longer.
But there was no avoiding it now.
Slowly, carefully, Aaravi nodded.
"Yes."
Mira's expression shifted, something between wonder and worry flickering across her features.
"Show me."
Aaravi hesitated. Her spirit animal had always been... different. Even among the Mayura tribeâ€”the peacock clan her family had belonged to for generationsâ€”her manifestation had never quite fit. Where others showed the traditional blue-green iridescence, where their tail feathers fanned in displays of conventional beauty, hers had always been more.
Too much.
Too bright.
Too other.
But if anyone would understand, it was Mira.
Aaravi closed her eyes and reached inward, toward the place where her spirit residedâ€”that quiet, ancient space that existed somewhere between her soul and the magic that flowed through all living things.

The change began in her chest.
A warmth that spread outward like ink in water, flowing through her veins, her bones, her very essence. Her skin prickled with familiar energy, and when she opened her eyes, she knew they would no longer be their usual dark brown.
They would be sapphire blue.
Glowing.

"Aaravi..."

The plumage spread behind her, each feather easily as long as her arm, each one tipped with an eye-spot that gleamed with an inner light. They didn't simply reflect the sun; they captured it, held it, transformed it into something altogether more radiant.
Aaravi's breath came shallow as the transformation settled into place.
This was her spirit form.
Not fully manifestedâ€”she was still mostly human, still herselfâ€”but enough to show the truth of what she was.
A peacock.
But not like any the Mayura tribe had ever seen.
Mira stared, her usually composed expression cracked with genuine awe.
"By the gods," she whispered. "You're not just Mayura bloodline. You're ancient Mayura. The original line."
Aaravi's throat tightened.
"What does that mean?"
Mira rose slowly, circling Aaravi to get a better view of the feathers. She reached out tentatively, her fingers hovering just above the plumage without quite touching.

Generations of intermarriage with other tribes, with humans who carried no spirit animal at all. The gifts weakened. The manifestations became smaller, simpler."
She paused, her gaze still fixed on the gold-veined feathers.
"But every few centuries, someone is born who carries the original bloodline. Undiluted. Pure. These individuals don't just manifest their spirit animalâ€”they embody it. They become legends."

"And what happened to her?" Aaravi asked, though part of her wasn't sure she wanted to know.
Mira's expression darkened.
"She died young. Twenty-six years old. The power burned through her like a wildfire, consuming everything in its path. They say she gave too much, healed too freely, never learned to guard what was hers." Mira finally met Aaravi's eyes. "Does that sound familiar?"
The words struck home with brutal accuracy.
Aaravi looked away, her hands curling into fists in her lap.
"I'm not her."
"No," Mira agreed. "But you could be. If you're not careful."
The feathers began to fade, dissolving back into light before disappearing entirely. Aaravi's eyes returned to their natural brown, and the warmth in her chest receded to its usual quiet hum.
She felt exhausted.
Manifesting always took energy, but this... this felt different. Heavier. As if the magic itself was trying to tell her something.

Mira sat back down, her expression grave.
"Because you've been Called."
Aaravi's blood ran cold.
"Called?"

The flames. The smoke. The way the fire had seemed to bend around him, as if it recognized him as kin rather than prey.
And his eyes.
Those burning, molten gold eyes.

Aaravi's mind raced through the possibilities.

But fire...
Fire belonged to only one.
"Garuda," she breathed. "The eagle tribe."
But even as she said it, she knew it wasn't quite right.
The eagles were warriors, yes. Fierce and powerful. But their fire was controlled, tempered. They were strategists, tacticians.
The fire she'd seen in her dreams was wild.
Consuming.
Reborn from its own destruction.
Mira's lips curved into a knowing smile.
"Not Garuda," she corrected gently. "Phoenix."
The word hung in the air between them, heavy with implication.
Phoenix.
The rarest of all fire-spirit manifestations.

Scholars debated whether it had ever truly existed at all, or if it was merely legendâ€”a symbolic representation of the Garuda tribe's most powerful warriors.

Seen the way the flames had bent to his will.
Seen the golden eyes that held the memory of a thousand deaths and rebirths.

"I don't understand. Why me? Why now?"

Aaravi's eyes stung with unshed tears.
"I'm afraid," she admitted. "What if I'm not strong enough? What if I can'tâ€”"

Finally, Aaravi spoke.
"What do I do?"
Mira smiled, something sad and proud in equal measure.
"You wait. And when he arrivesâ€”because he will arriveâ€”you answer the call. Not because fate demands it, but because you choose it."


Aaravi spent the rest of the day trying to focus on her work and failing spectacularly.
She brewed three more incorrect tinctures before Mira physically removed the mortar and pestle from her hands and sent her to the meditation gardens.
"Go," the older woman had said, not unkindly. "Sit. Breathe. Center yourself. You're no good to anyone like this."
So Aaravi had gone.
She sat now beneath the ancient banyan tree at the heart of the meditation gardens, her back against its massive trunk, her eyes closed. The tree was said to be over three hundred years old, its roots spreading so deep and wide that they connected to the very bones of the earth.
Sitting here usually brought clarity.
But today, all it brought was more questions.
Phoenix and Peacock.
The words circled in her mind like birds of prey.
She reached inward again, toward her spirit, and this time she didn't pull back. She let the transformation wash over her fullyâ€”not just the feathers, but everything.
Her bones lightened, hollowing just enough to make her feel weightless. Her vision sharpened, colors becoming more vibrant, details more precise. Her hearing expanded, picking up the rustle of leaves, the whisper of wind, the distant murmur of voices from the temple.
And deep in her chest, where her human heart beat, she felt something else stirring.
Her spirit's heart.
It pulsed in rhythm with her own, but also... not quite. As if it were beating to a drum she couldn't fully hear yet.
As if it were waiting for something.
Someone.
Find me, she had whispered in the dream.
And now, sitting beneath the ancient tree with her spirit fully awake, she whispered it again.
"I'm here. Come find me."
The words barely left her lips when she felt it.
A pull.
Sharp and sudden, like a rope snapping taut.
South.
He was to the south.
Moving toward her.
Aaravi's eyes flew open, her breath catching.
She didn't know how she knew. Couldn't explain the certainty that flooded through her veins like liquid fire.
But she knew.
He was coming.
The Phoenix was coming.
And when he arrived, everything would change.


That evening, Aaravi stood on the highest terrace of the temple, watching the sun sink toward the horizon.
Her father found her there, as she somehow knew he would.
Ravindra said nothing at first, simply stood beside her, his presence a quiet comfort.
Finally, he spoke.
"Mira told me."
Aaravi glanced at him. "Told you what?"
"That you're ancient bloodline. That you've been Called." He paused, his expression pained. "That you'll likely leave us soon."
Aaravi's throat tightened.
"I don't know what's going to happen," she admitted. "I don't know if I'll leave or if he'll stay or ifâ€”"
"You'll go where you need to go," Ravindra interrupted gently. "And I'll support you. Whatever you choose."
He turned to face her fully, his hands settling on her shoulders.
"You've spent your entire life healing others, Aaravi. Taking care of everyone around you. Never asking for anything in return." His eyes shone with unshed tears. "Maybe it's time someone took care of you for a change."
The words broke something open inside her.
Aaravi stepped forward, wrapping her arms around her father, and for the first time in longer than she could remember, she let herself cry.
Not from sadness.
But from relief.
Because for the first time in her life, she was going to let herself want something.
Not for duty.
Not for family.
But for herself.
And when the Phoenix arrivedâ€”when those golden eyes finally found her in the waking world instead of dreamsâ€”she would be ready.
She would answer the call.
Not because fate demanded it.
But because she chose it.



CHAPTER FIVE: THE ROAD OF FIRE
ðŸ”¥   âœ¨
  
The road south was older than memory.
Vihan's horseâ€”a sturdy grey mare named Ashvaâ€”picked her way carefully along the ancient stone path, her hooves finding purchase where centuries of travelers had worn the rock smooth. The forest pressed close on either side, dense and watchful, the canopy overhead filtering the morning sun into scattered coins of gold.
Vihan had been riding for three days.
Three days since he'd left the camp, left Varun and the carefully ordered world of military discipline behind. Three days of traveling alone with nothing but his thoughts, his sword, and the increasingly insistent pull in his chest that urged him forward.
South. Go south.
He didn't question it anymore.
Couldn't, even if he wanted to.
The pull had become as essential as breathing, a compass needle pointing toward something he couldn't see but knewâ€”knewâ€”was there.
Was her.
Ashva snorted, tossing her head, and Vihan automatically loosened his grip on the reins. He'd been clenching them without realizing it, his knuckles white, his jaw tight.
"Easy, girl," he murmured, reaching down to pat her neck. "I know. I'm distracted."
The mare's ears flicked back toward him, as if to say distracted is putting it mildly.
Vihan allowed himself a faint smile.
Ashva was Garuda-touchedâ€”not a spirit bearer herself, but descended from horses bred by the eagle clans for generations. She was faster than normal horses, more intuitive, and possessed an almost uncanny ability to sense danger before it arrived.
Which was why, when she suddenly stopped dead in the middle of the path, her ears pinned flat against her skull, Vihan's hand went immediately to his sword.
"What is it?"
Ashva's nostrils flared, her entire body going rigid.
Vihan's eyes scanned the forest, searching for movement, for the telltale signs of an ambush. But the trees were still. The birds had fallen silent. Even the wind seemed to have paused, holding its breath.
And then he felt it.
A presence.
Not human.
Not entirely.
The air grew heavier, pressing down on his shoulders like an invisible weight. His chest tightened, and deep insideâ€”deeper than bone, deeper than bloodâ€”his spirit stirred.
The Phoenix.
It had been quiet since he'd left the camp, dormant in that space between waking and sleeping where spirit animals resided when not actively manifested. But now it moved, stretching like a cat waking from a long nap, its awareness bleeding into his own.
Danger, it seemed to say. Predator.
Vihan's eyes narrowed.
"Show yourself," he called out, his voice carrying through the silent forest.

A wolf.
But not a natural one.
It was massiveâ€”easily the size of a small horseâ€”its fur the color of smoke and ash, its eyes burning with an intelligence that was distinctly, unsettlingly human. A Vrika.
Wolf clan.
And from the size and the aura of power rolling off it in waves, this was no common pack member. This was a warrior. Possibly an alpha.
The wolf padded forward slowly, its gaze locked on Vihan with predatory focus. It didn't attack immediatelyâ€”that wasn't the Vrika way. They assessed first. Measured. Decided whether their prey was worth the effort.

The wolf's lips pulled back, revealing teeth like daggers.

Still wolf. Still burning with that predatory intelligence.

"Garuda?" the wolf-man asked, his voice rough, as if human speech was something he had to remember how to do.

The man's eyes widened fractionally.

"Not quite."

Mayura.
Peacock tribe.
Her tribe.

"My mate."
The effect was immediate.
The wolf-man's entire demeanor shifted. The predatory edge didn't disappearâ€”wolves were never truly safeâ€”but it... softened. Changed. Became something more like respect.
"Soul-called?" he asked quietly.
Vihan nodded.
The wolf-man exhaled, something almost like wonder crossing his features.
"I've lived forty-three years and never met a bonded pair. The old ones in my pack talk about them like legends. Like myths." He stepped back, giving Vihan space. "You're either blessed or cursed, Phoenix. I haven't decided which."
"Neither have I," Vihan admitted.
The man laughed again, but this time it held genuine humor.
"I'm Ruhaan," he offered. "Alpha of the Ashfall pack. These woods are our hunting grounds." He gestured to the forest around them. "Normally, I'd challenge you. Territory and pride, you understand. But..." He paused. "Soul-called bonds are sacred. Even among wolves. I won't stand in your way."
Vihan felt some of the tension leave his shoulders.

Ruhaan nodded, then tilted his head, considering.
"A warning, though, Phoenix. The road ahead isn't safe. There are... things stirring. Dark things. Bandits, yes, but also worse. Rogues. Exiles. Vihan's blood ran cold.
Sharvani.

Ruhaan's eyes narrowed.

CHAPTER SEVEN: WHEN FIRE MEETS FATE

VIHAN
Dawn broke over the mountains like a promise.

He'd given up on rest around the third hour before dawn, instead packing his supplies with mechanical precision and saddling Ashva in the pre-dawn darkness.

Toward her.

CHAPTER NINE: LEARNING TO STAND STILL

Too peaceful.
Vihan's hands gripped the windowsill, his knuckles white.
He'd barely slept. Every time he closed his eyes, his body tensed, waiting for attack. Waiting for the alarm that would send him scrambling for his sword, for the shouts of guards, for the clash of steel that had been the soundtrack of his life for more years than he could count.
But the alarm never came.
There were no threats here. No enemies at the gates. No battles to plan or wars to fight.
Just... peace.
And gods help him, he didn't know what to do with it.
His spirit prowled restlessly beneath his skin, the Phoenix agitated by the stillness. It wanted to move, to burn, to do something other than simply exist in this tranquil sanctuary.
Calm down, Vihan told it silently. We're safe here.
But the Phoenix didn't understand safety. It only understood fire and fury and the constant motion of survival.
A soft knock at the door made him turn.
"Come in."
The door opened to reveal Aaravi, dressed in simple grey robes, her hair braided over one shoulder. She carried a tray with tea and what looked like flatbread and fruit.
"I thought you might be awake," she said, stepping inside. "You feel... unsettled."
The bond. Of course she could feel it.
Vihan tried to smooth the turbulence inside him, to calm the restless energy, but it was like trying to hold back a tide with his bare hands.
"I'm fine," he said automatically.
Aaravi set the tray on the small table by the window and turned to face him fully. Her dark eyes were knowing, gentle, and completely unfooled.
"You're not fine. You've been awake for hours, pacing like a caged animal." She paused. "I could feel it. Through the bond. Like a storm brewing."
Vihan's jaw tightened.
"I'm sorry. I didn't mean to disturb you."
"You didn't disturb me. You woke me. There's a difference." Aaravi moved to stand beside him at the window. "Talk to me. What's wrong?"
Everything and nothing.
How did he explain that peace felt more dangerous than war? That stillness made him more anxious than any battlefield? That he'd spent so long being a weapon that he didn't remember how to simply be a person?
"I don't know how to do this," Vihan admitted finally, his voice rough. "How to just... exist. Without purpose. Without a fight to prepare for or an enemy to watch for."
Aaravi was quiet for a moment, her gaze on the valley below.
"You think peace means purposelessness."
It wasn't a question.
"Doesn't it?" Vihan's hands clenched at his sides. "I've been a soldier since I was twelve years old. Everything I amâ€”everything I know how to beâ€”is wrapped up in that. Strategy. Combat. Survival. Take that away and what's left?"
"You," Aaravi said simply. "What's left is you. Not the weapon. Not the warrior. Just Vihan."
The words landed like stones in still water.
"I don't know who that is."
Aaravi turned to him, her expression soft with understanding.
"Then maybe it's time you found out." She reached for his hand, her fingers warm against his. "Come with me."


She led him through the temple to the gardens.
Not the formal courtyard where they'd first met, but a smaller, more intimate space tucked behind the main buildings. Here, raised beds overflowed with herbs and medicinal plants, their leaves glistening with morning dew. Vihan looked around, taking in the carefully tended plants, the orderly rows, the sense of deliberate cultivation.
"It's beautiful."

Vihan met her gaze.
"Teach me what?"
"How to be still."
The words were so simple, so impossible, that Vihan almost laughed.
"I don't think that's something that can be taught."
"Then let me show you." Aaravi moved to one of the raised beds, kneeling beside a cluster of basil plants. "Come here."
Vihan followed slowly, settling onto his knees beside her. The position felt strangeâ€”vulnerable, somehow. Warriors didn't kneel unless they were injured or surrendering.
But this was neither.
"Give me your hands," Aaravi said softly.
He did, and she placed them gently on the soil beside the basil.
"Feel that?"
The earth was cool and slightly damp, rich and dark. He could feel the tiny granules of soil between his fingers, the slight give of the ground beneath his palms.
"Yes."
"That's real," Aaravi said. "Not strategy. Not combat. Just earth. Just life growing from soil and water and sunlight." She moved his hands to touch the basil leaves. "Feel this."
The leaves were soft, slightly fuzzy, and when his fingers brushed them, they released a sharp, clean scent.
"Smell that?" Aaravi asked.
He nodded, breathing it in.
"That's basil. Holy basil, specifically. We use it for tea, for healing inflammation, for calming anxiety." Her voice was gentle, hypnotic almost. "But right now, it's not medicine. It's just a plant. Growing. Existing. Not because it has to fight or survive or prove its worth. Just... being."
Vihan stared at the small green leaves under his scarred, calloused hands.
"I don't understand."
"You don't have to understand. You just have to feel." Aaravi's hand covered his, warm and grounding. "You've spent so long movingâ€”fighting, running, survivingâ€”that you've forgotten how to simply exist in a moment. Without purpose beyond the moment itself."
"That sounds like weakness," Vihan said quietly.
"It's not weakness. It's peace." Aaravi's thumb traced gentle circles on the back of his hand. "And peace isn't the absence of strength, Vihan. It's the presence of balance. The ability to choose stillness when you could choose violence. The courage to be vulnerable when you could be guarded."
Vihan closed his eyes, trying to let her words sink in.
It went against every instinct, every lesson he'd learned in decades of warfare.
But beneath his palms, the basil continued to grow. Unconcerned with his turmoil. Simply existing, as Aaravi said. Being.
Slowlyâ€”so slowlyâ€”he felt something in his chest begin to loosen.
Not the Phoenix. That was still restless, still prowling.
But something deeper. Something human.
"How long did it take you to learn this?" he asked.
Aaravi's laugh was soft and slightly sad.
"I'm still learning. But I had to start somewhere. And so do you."
They stayed like that for a long time, kneeling in the garden, their hands on the earth, the morning sun warming their backs.
And for the first time in longer than Vihan could remember, his mind... quieted.
Not silent. Not empty.
But calmer. Like a storm gradually spending itself.
"Thank you," he murmured.
Aaravi squeezed his hand.
"You don't have to thank me. We're learning together, remember?"


AARAVI
By midmorning, Aaravi had coaxed Vihan into helping her tend the gardens.
He was terrible at it.
Not because he was unskilled with his handsâ€”quite the opposite. But because he approached every task like a military operation. The weeding became strategic extraction. The watering became a precisely calculated distribution of resources. The pruning looked more like a surgical strike than gentle cultivation.
Aaravi watched him attack a particularly stubborn weed with the same intensity he probably brought to combat, and she couldn't help but laugh.
"It's a plant, not an enemy soldier."
Vihan looked up, the weed dangling from his hand, dirt smudging his cheek.
"It was choking the sage."
"Yes, but you don't have to destroy it. Just remove it." Aaravi knelt beside him, demonstrating. "See? Gentle pressure at the base, then lift. The roots come out clean, and the surrounding plants aren't disturbed."
Vihan tried again, this time with more care.
The weed came free without incident.
"Better," Aaravi said, smiling.
He stared at the plant in his hand for a moment, then looked at her.
"Everything here is gentle. The way you move, the way you speak, the way you tend these plants. Like you're afraid of breaking something."

"And that applies to plants too."
"It applies to everything." Aaravi reached for another weed, her movements slow and deliberate. "People. Plants. Even..." She paused. "Even broken warriors who don't know how to be anything else."
Vihan's hands stilled.
"I'm not broken."

They returned to the weeding, working side by side in comfortable silence.

It was a small change.
But it was something.


After the gardens, Aaravi led him to the healing rooms, intending to show him some basic herbalism.

The elderly man's eyes widened, his hand moving instinctively toward his chest. The young woman shrank back slightly. Even the feverish child whimpered and turned away.

"I should go," he said quietly.

The elderly man was the first to speak, his voice scratchy.
"Phoenix hasn't been seen in these lands for generations."
"I know," Aaravi said. "He's rare. Powerful. But he's also kind. And he's trying to learn a different way of being."
She could feel Vihan's skepticism through the bond, his doubt that anyone would believe her.

"If you vouch for him, healer, then I trust your judgment."
The young woman still looked uncertain, but she didn't protest.
The child just stared, wide-eyed and curious now instead of afraid.

"See? You just have to give them a chance to adjust."
Vihan's jaw was tight, but he nodded stiffly.

What followed was a study in contrasts.
Aaravi moved through the healing room with practiced graceâ€”checking the elderly man's lungs, wrapping the young woman's ankle, placing cool cloths on the child's forehead. Every movement was efficient but gentle, professional but warm.
And Vihan watched.
At first, he stood apart, clearly uncomfortable with the intimacy of the healing space. But gradually, as the patients relaxed into Aaravi's care, he moved closer.
When she asked him to hand her a particular herb, he did.
When she needed help repositioning the elderly man, he stepped forward without hesitation, his movements surprisingly careful.
And when the child started crying from the discomfort of her fever, Vihan did something that made Aaravi's heart clench.

"It hurts, doesn't it?"
The child nodded, tears streaming down her cheeks.
"I know. But the healerâ€”" He gestured to Aaravi. "She's going to make it better. You just have to be brave a little longer. Can you do that?"
The child studied him with fever-bright eyes.
"You're the fire man."
Vihan blinked. "Yes."

"Never," Vihan said firmly. "I only burn bad things. And you're not bad. You're just sick. And sick things get better."
The child considered this, then held out her small hand.
"Can I see?"
Vihan glanced at Aaravi, uncertain.
She nodded encouragingly.
Slowly, carefully, Vihan took the child's hand in his much larger one. And then, with visible effort, he let a tiny bit of the Phoenix through.
Just enough that his eyes glowed faintly gold. Just enough that warmthâ€”gentle, controlled warmth, like sun-warmed stonesâ€”radiated from his palm into the child's hand.
The girl's eyes widened with wonder.
"Pretty," she whispered.
Then she smiled and settled back onto her pillow, the fear gone from her expression.
Vihan released her hand gently and rose, his movements stiff with controlled emotion.
Aaravi finished her work in silence, but she could feel the bond humming with something complexâ€”relief, wonder, a fragile kind of hope.
When they finally left the healing room, Vihan stopped in the hallway.
"She wasn't afraid of me."
"No," Aaravi agreed softly. "She wasn't. Because you showed her you weren't a threat. You showed her gentleness."
"I didn't know I still could."
Aaravi took his hand, threading her fingers through his.
"Then I'm glad you're remembering."


VIHAN
That afternoon, Aaravi kept her promise.
She took him to the temple's training groundsâ€”a wide, open space behind the main buildings where the younger residents practiced meditation and movement exercises.
Currently, it was empty.
"You wanted to learn to fight," Vihan said, surveying the space. "This isn't much of a battlefield."
"It's not supposed to be a battlefield. It's supposed to be a practice ground." Aaravi moved to the center of the space, turning to face him. "Show me what you know."
"What I know would hurt you."

Vihan's jaw tightened.
He wasn't afraid of that.
Was he?
"Alright." He moved into a ready stance, weight balanced, hands loose. "First lesson: awareness. You need to know where your body is in space at all times. Where your balance is. Where you're vulnerable."
He demonstrated, shifting his weight, showing her how to stand.

"Good," Vihan said. "Nowâ€”"
He moved.
Not fast. Not with the killing intent he'd use in real combat. But enough to test her.

"No." Vihan's mind raced. "No, that was good. That was perfect."
"What?"
"Your instinct isn't to fight physically. It's to use your spirit. Your light." He circled her slowly, thinking. "Most people train their bodies first and their spirits second. But you've been training your spirit your whole life through healing. So we approach this differently."
"How?"
"We make your light a weapon."
Aaravi's brow furrowed.
"Light doesn't harm. It heals."
"Light blinds. Light reveals. Light drives back darkness." Vihan stopped in front of her. "And in combat, those are powerful tools. You don't need to hurt your enemies. You just need to stop them. Disorient them. Give yourself time to escape or for help to arrive."
Understanding dawned in Aaravi's eyes.
"So instead of learning to punch and kickâ€”"
"We learn to use what you already have. Your spirit. Your light. Your healing knowledge." Vihan's lips curved. "There are pressure points that cause unconsciousness. Herbs that induce sleep. Ways to use your gifts defensively that don't require you to kill."

"I'd teach you to survive," Vihan said seriously. "By any means necessary. But in a way that doesn't betray who you are."
Aaravi stared at him for a long moment.
Then she smiledâ€”bright and genuine and so beautiful it made his chest ache.

They trained for two hours.
Vihan taught her how to channel light through her hands in quick burstsâ€”enough to disorient an attacker. How to use her small size to her advantage, slipping away from grapples instead of trying to overpower. How to target vulnerable pointsâ€”eyes, throat, jointsâ€”if she absolutely had to.
And Aaravi learned.
Faster than he expected. With a focus and determination that reminded him she might be gentle, but she wasn't weak.
By the time the sun began its descent, they were both sweating, both laughing, both more relaxed than they'd been since the bond formed.
"Enough for today," Vihan said finally. "You'll be sore tomorrow."
"Worth it." Aaravi wiped sweat from her forehead. "Thank you. For teaching me. And for..." She paused. "For not treating me like I'm fragile."
"You're not fragile," Vihan said. "You're strong. You just needed to be shown how to apply that strength differently."
They walked back toward the temple together, the bond humming contentedly between them.
And for the first time since arriving, Vihan felt like maybeâ€”just maybeâ€”he could learn to exist in peace.
Not perfectly.
Not without struggle.
But with help.
With her.


That night, Vihan dreamed.
But not of Sharvani.
Not of fire and fury and battlefields soaked in blood.
He dreamed of a garden.
Of kneeling beside Aaravi, their hands in the earth, sunlight warm on their backs.
Of peace.
And when he woke, just before dawn, the Phoenix was... quieter.
Still present. Still powerful.
But no longer quite so desperate to burn.
He rose and went to the window, looking out at the valley painted in pre-dawn grey.
And he didn't see a battlefield to defend.
He saw a home to protect.
There was a difference.
One he was only just beginning to understand.


But in the forest beyond the valley, hidden among shadows and ancient trees, something watched.
A presence, neither fully physical nor fully spirit, observing the temple with hollow, hungry eyes.
It had felt the bond form.
Felt the surge of power that came from a Phoenix and a Peacock joining.
And it wanted that power for itself.
Or, failing that, it wanted to destroy it.
The shadow smiledâ€”a terrible, predatory expressionâ€”and melted deeper into the darkness.
Waiting.
Planning.
Soon.



CHAPTER TEN: THE SHADOW STIRS

SHARVANI
The war room smelled of incense and ambition.
Sharvani stood at the center of the circular chamber, her hands braced on the massive stone table that dominated the space. Maps covered its surfaceâ€”territories marked in red, trade routes traced in black, potential allies and enemies denoted by different colored pins.
An empire in the making.
Her empire.
But the maps might as well have been blank parchment for all the attention she paid them now.
Because three days ago, she had felt it.
The bond.
Even now, the memory of that moment made her chest constrict with something that might have been pain if Sharvani allowed herself to feel such weakness.
She had been meditating in her private chamberâ€”a daily ritual she maintained to keep her Lioness spirit sharp and focusedâ€”when the surge had hit her. A wave of power so immense, so bright, that it had shattered her concentration and sent her sprawling onto the stone floor.

Phoenix and Peacock.
Vihan.
And some insignificant temple girl who had no rightâ€”no rightâ€”to touch what belonged to Sharvani.
Her hands clenched on the edge of the table, claws extending slightly, scoring the stone.

"Speak, Daksh."
Her second-in-command moved forward, his footsteps careful, measured. He'd learned long ago that approaching the Lioness when she was in a mood required caution.

Then: "The Phoenix has bonded. Soul-bond. With a Mayura from the temple lands."
The words hung in the air like an executioner's blade.
Sharvani closed her eyes, forcing herself to breathe slowly, deliberately. Control was everything. Emotion was weakness. She had built her empire on iron will and calculated strategy, not on the messy, chaotic feelings that plagued lesser beings.
But gods, it was hard to maintain control when every instinct screamed at her to burn that temple to the ground.
"Details," she said finally, her voice steady despite the fury coiling in her gut.
Daksh consulted a rolled parchment, though Sharvani suspected he had the information memorized. He was nothing if not thorough.

Sharvani's eyes snapped open.
"Ancient?"
"Yes, my lady. The scouts report that her feathers aren't the common blue-green. They're pure sapphire threaded with gold. The temple elders are calling her the first true ancient Mayura in three generations."
Of course.
Of course it would be an ancient bloodline.
Because Vihan deserved nothing less than perfection, didn't he? Even when he was being a fool, even when he was running from his destiny, even when he was betraying everything they had built togetherâ€”he still managed to find someone worthy of his power.

"What else?" she asked, her voice carefully neutral.
"The bond formed four days ago. The surge was felt as far north as the Ashfall pack territories. Several tribes sent envoys to the temple, acknowledging the bond and offering... protection."
Sharvani's claws extended fully, piercing the stone table.
Protection.
As if Vihan needed protection from her.
As if she were some common threat instead of the woman who had made him everything he was.
"The Forgotten Order?" she asked, already knowing the answer.
"Sent word. They're en route but won't arrive for another week. The temple is relying on their own defenses and the goodwill of neighboring tribes in the meantime."
A week.
That gave her time.
Not much, but enough.
"And Vihan?" Sharvani's voice softened, despite herself. "What do the scouts report about him?"
Daksh hesitated.
That told her everything.
"He's..." Daksh chose his words carefully. "He's adapting. To the temple. To peace. The scouts report he's been helping in the gardens, assisting in the healing rooms. Training the Peacock in self-defense."
Each word was a knife between Sharvani's ribs.
Gardens. Healing rooms. Peace.
Vihanâ€”her magnificent, terrible Phoenixâ€”was playing at domesticity like some common farmer.
The rage that swept through her was so intense that her Lioness manifested partially, golden fur rippling across her arms, her eyes shifting to their hollow, predatory amber.
Daksh took a step back.
Wise man.
Sharvani forced the transformation to recede, pulled her spirit back under control through sheer force of will.
"Leave me," she said quietly.
"My ladyâ€”"
"Leave."
Daksh bowed and retreated, the door closing behind him with a soft click.
Alone now, Sharvani allowed herself a moment of weakness.
She sank into the chair at the head of the table, her hands covering her face, her breathing ragged.
Vihan. My Vihan. What have you done?


FIFTEEN YEARS AGO
Sharvani had been nothing when she found him.
No, that wasn't quite right. She'd been worse than nothing. She'd been the daughter of exiles, born into a tribe that had cast out her parents for the crime of loving across bloodlinesâ€”Lion and Eagle, predator and sky-dweller, two spirits that were never meant to mix.
The union had produced her. A child with a Lioness spirit but Eagle cunning. Powerful but unwelcome. Belonging nowhere.
Her parents had died when she was ten. Fever, the village healers said. But Sharvani had always suspected poison. The tribe didn't like reminders of their own prejudice.
So she'd survived alone.
Learned to hunt. Learned to fight. Learned that power was the only currency that mattered, the only thing that kept predators at bay.
By the time she was twenty, she'd carved out a territory of her own. Small. Barely enough to sustain her. But hers.
And then Vihan walked into her camp.
He'd been seventeen. Tall for his age but too thin, all sharp angles and hungry eyes. His clothes were little more than rags, and dried blood caked his knuckles.
He'd collapsed at her fire without invitation, without apology, and looked at her with those molten gold eyes.
"I can fight," he'd said. "I can hunt. I can kill. I just need somewhere to stay. Someone to..." He'd trailed off, his jaw tightening. "Someone who won't try to break me."
Sharvani had studied himâ€”this ragged boy with a Phoenix spirit barely contained beneath his skinâ€”and seen potential.
Not just power. Not just strength.
But hunger.
The same hunger that burned in her own chest. The desperation to be more than what the world said they could be.
"What's your name?" she'd asked.
"Vihan."
"And why are you running, Vihan?"
His expression had shuttered.
"Does it matter?"
"It matters if you're bringing trouble to my door."
He'd been silent for a long moment. Then: "My father sold me to a warlord. Said a Phoenix spirit was worth more than a son. I killed the warlord's guards and ran."
Simple. Brutal. Honest.
Sharvani had liked him immediately.
"You can stay," she'd said. "But if you're going to be here, you're going to be useful. I'll train you. Make you stronger. Better. And in exchange, you help me build something."
"Build what?"
She'd smiled, showing teeth.
"Everything."
And gods help her, they had built something.
Over the next ten years, they'd carved out territory after territory. She'd taught him strategy, diplomacy, the art of turning enemies into allies. He'd taught her combat, direct action, the kind of decisive violence that made people think twice before challenging them.
They'd been magnificent together.
And somewhere along the way, Sharvani had stopped seeing him as a tool and started seeing him as... something else.
A partner.
An equal.
Hers.
She'd never loved anything before Vihan. Hadn't known how. But what she felt for himâ€”this fierce, possessive, all-consuming needâ€”that had to be love, didn't it?
So she'd married him.
Not with vows or witnesses or any of that sentimental nonsense. But with action. With claiming. With making it clear to every person in their growing territory that the Phoenix belonged to the Lioness, and anyone who thought otherwise would answer to her claws.
And for a whileâ€”for a few glorious yearsâ€”it had been perfect.
They'd been unstoppable. The Phoenix and the Lioness, fire and fury, building an empire from blood and ambition.
Until he'd changed.


PRESENT
Sharvani didn't know when it started.
The subtle shifts in Vihan's demeanor. The way he started questioning her decisions. The moments he pulled away when she reached for him.
At first, she'd dismissed it. Stress. The weight of command. Everyone wavered sometimes.
But it got worse.
He started spending less time with her. More time alone. He'd stare at the horizon like he was searching for something she couldn't see.
And then, three years ago, he'd said the words that shattered everything.

Just like that. No preamble. No explanation.
"What?"
"I'm leaving, Sharvani. Thisâ€”" He'd gestured at the war room, the maps, the empire. "â€”this isn't what I want anymore."
She'd laughed. Actually laughed. Because it was absurd.
